      <p><b>Liechtenstein</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ən/: 'on' in 'button'">ən</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Liechtenstein.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1b/En-us-Liechtenstein.ogg" class="internal" title="En-us-Liechtenstein.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">LIK</span>-tən-styne</i></a>; <small>German: </small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈlɪçtn̩ʃtaɪn]</a></span>), officially the <b>Principality of Liechtenstein</b> (German: <i lang="de">Fürstentum Liechtenstein</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German-speaking</a> <a href="/wiki/Microstate" title="Microstate">microstate</a> situated in the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a> between <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> in Central Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Principality" title="Principality">principality</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Semi-constitutional_monarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="Semi-constitutional monarchy">semi-constitutional monarchy</a> headed by the <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Liechtenstein" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince of Liechtenstein">Prince of Liechtenstein</a>; the Prince's extensive powers are equivalent to those of a President in a <a href="/wiki/Semi-presidential_system" title="Semi-presidential system">semi-presidential system</a>. </p>
      <p>Liechtenstein is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> to the west and south and <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> to the east and north. It is Europe's fourth-smallest country, with an area of just over 160 square kilometres (62 square miles) and a population of 38,749.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Divided into <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Liechtenstein" title="Municipalities of Liechtenstein">11 municipalities</a>, its capital is <a href="/wiki/Vaduz" title="Vaduz">Vaduz</a>, and its largest municipality is <a href="/wiki/Schaan" title="Schaan">Schaan</a>. It is also the smallest country to border two countries.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Liechtenstein is one of only two <a href="/wiki/Landlocked_country#Doubly_landlocked" title="Landlocked country">doubly landlocked</a> countries in the world. </p>
      <p>Economically, Liechtenstein has one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">highest gross domestic products per person in the world</a> when adjusted for <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The country has a strong <a href="/wiki/Financial_services" title="Financial services">financial sector</a> centred in Vaduz. It was once known as a billionaire <a href="/wiki/Tax_haven" title="Tax haven">tax haven</a>, but is no longer on any official blacklists of uncooperative tax haven countries. An <a href="/wiki/Alpine_states" title="Alpine states">Alpine country</a>, Liechtenstein is mountainous, making it a winter sport destination. </p>
      <p>Liechtenstein is a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, the <a href="/wiki/European_Free_Trade_Association" title="European Free Trade Association">European Free Trade Association</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a>. Although not a member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, it participates in both the <a href="/wiki/Schengen_Area" title="Schengen Area">Schengen Area</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a>. It has a <a href="/wiki/Customs_union" title="Customs union">customs union</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Monetary_union" class="mw-redirect" title="Monetary union">monetary union</a> with Switzerland. </p>

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Johann_Josef_I_von_Liechtenstein.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Johann_Josef_I_von_Liechtenstein.jpg/170px-Johann_Josef_I_von_Liechtenstein.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Johann_Josef_I_von_Liechtenstein.jpg/255px-Johann_Josef_I_von_Liechtenstein.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Johann_Josef_I_von_Liechtenstein.jpg/340px-Johann_Josef_I_von_Liechtenstein.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2183" data-file-height="2750"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Johann_Josef_I_von_Liechtenstein.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Johann_I_Joseph,_Prince_of_Liechtenstein" class="mw-redirect" title="Johann I Joseph, Prince of Liechtenstein">Johann I Joseph, Prince of Liechtenstein</a> from 1805 to 1806 and 1814 to 1836, by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Baptist_von_Lampi_the_Elder" title="Johann Baptist von Lampi the Elder">Johann Baptist von Lampi the Elder</a>. <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_Museum" title="Liechtenstein Museum">Liechtenstein Museum</a>, Vienna
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The oldest traces of human existence in the area of present-day Liechtenstein date back to the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic" title="Middle Paleolithic">Middle Paleolithic</a> era.<sup id="cite_ref-Early_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Early-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> farming settlements appeared in the valleys around 5300 BC. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt_culture" title="Hallstatt culture"> Hallstatt</a> and <a href="/wiki/La_T%C3%A8ne_culture" title="La Tène culture">La Tène cultures</a> flourished during the late <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>, from around 450 BC—possibly under some influence of both the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek"> Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_civilization" title="Etruscan civilization">Etruscan</a> civilisations. One of the most important tribal groups in the Alpine region were the <a href="/wiki/Helvetii" title="Helvetii">Helvetii</a>. In 58 BC, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bibracte" title="Battle of Bibracte">Battle of Bibracte</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> defeated the Alpine tribes, thereby bringing the region under close control of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>. By 15 BC, <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a> — later the second Roman emperor — with his brother, <a href="/wiki/Nero_Claudius_Drusus" title="Nero Claudius Drusus"> Drusus</a>, conquered the entirety of the Alpine area. Liechtenstein then became integrated into the <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">Roman province</a> of <a href="/wiki/Raetia" title="Raetia">Raetia</a>. The area was maintained<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> by the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Roman_army" title="Imperial Roman army"> Roman army</a>, which maintained large legionary camps at <a href="/wiki/Bregenz" title="Bregenz"> Brigantium</a> (Austria), near <a href="/wiki/Lake_Constance" title="Lake Constance">Lake Constance</a>, and at <a href="/wiki/Maienfeld" title="Maienfeld">Magia</a> (Swiss). The Romans built and maintained a <a href="/wiki/Roman_road" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman road"> road</a> which ran through the territory. Circa AD 260 Brigantium was destroyed by the <a href="/wiki/Alemanni" title="Alemanni">Alemanni</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Germanic_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic people">Germanic people</a> who settled in the area in around AD 450. </p>
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>, the Alemanni settled the eastern <a href="/wiki/Swiss_plateau" class="mw-redirect" title="Swiss plateau">Swiss plateau</a> by the 5th century and the <a href="/wiki/Valleys_of_the_Alps" class="mw-redirect" title="Valleys of the Alps">valleys of the Alps</a> by the end of the 8th century, with Liechtenstein located at the eastern edge of <a href="/wiki/Alamannia" title="Alamannia"> Alemannia</a>. In the 6th century the entire region became part of the <a href="/wiki/Frankish_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Frankish Empire">Frankish Empire</a> following <a href="/wiki/Clovis_I" title="Clovis I"> Clovis&nbsp;I</a>'s victory over the Alemanni at <a href="/wiki/Tolbiac" class="mw-redirect" title="Tolbiac">Tolbiac</a> in AD 504.<sup id="cite_ref-Nationsencyclopedia_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nationsencyclopedia-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nationsonline_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nationsonline-16">[16]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The area that later became Liechtenstein remained under Frankish hegemony (<a href="/wiki/Merovingians" class="mw-redirect" title="Merovingians">Merovingian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire"> Carolingian</a> dynasties) until the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Verdun" title="Treaty of Verdun">Treaty of Verdun</a> divided the Carolingian empire in AD 843, following the death of <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> in 814.<sup id="cite_ref-Early_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Early-14">[14]</a></sup> The territory of present-day Liechtenstein formed part of <a href="/wiki/East_Francia" title="East Francia">East Francia</a>. It would later be reunified with <a href="/wiki/Middle_Francia" title="Middle Francia">Middle Francia</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, around AD 1000.<sup id="cite_ref-Early_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Early-14">[14]</a></sup> Until about 1100 the predominant language of the area was <a href="/wiki/Romansh_language" title="Romansh language"> Romansch</a>, but thereafter <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language"> German</a> began to gain ground in the territory. In 1300 another Alemannic population — the <a href="/wiki/Walser" title="Walser">Walsers</a>, who originated in <a href="/wiki/Valais" title="Valais">Valais</a> — entered the region and settled; the mountain village of <a href="/wiki/Triesenberg" title="Triesenberg">Triesenberg</a> still preserves today features of <a href="/wiki/Walser_German" title="Walser German"> Walser dialect</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundation_of_a_dynasty">Foundation of a dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Foundation of a dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>By 1200, dominions across the Alpine plateau were controlled by the Houses of <a href="/wiki/House_of_Savoy" title="House of Savoy">Savoy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%A4hringer" class="mw-redirect" title="Zähringer">Zähringer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Habsburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Habsburg">Habsburg</a>, and <a href="/wiki/House_of_Kyburg" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Kyburg">Kyburg</a>. Other regions were accorded the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_immediacy" title="Imperial immediacy">Imperial immediacy</a> that granted the empire direct control over the mountain passes. When the Kyburg dynasty fell in 1264, the Habsburgs under <a href="/wiki/Rudolph_I_of_Habsburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Rudolph I of Habsburg">King Rudolph I</a> (Holy Roman Emperor in 1273) extended their territory to the eastern Alpine plateau that included the territory of Liechtenstein.<sup id="cite_ref-Nationsencyclopedia_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nationsencyclopedia-15">[15]</a></sup> This region was <a href="/wiki/Feoffment" title="Feoffment">enfeoffed</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Hohenems" title="Hohenems">Counts of Hohenems</a> until the sale to the <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Liechtenstein dynasty">Liechtenstein dynasty</a> in 1699. </p>
      <p>In 1396 <a href="/wiki/Vaduz" title="Vaduz">Vaduz</a> (the southern region of Liechtenstein) gained <a href="/wiki/Imperial_immediacy" title="Imperial immediacy">imperial immediacy</a>, i.e. it became subject to the Holy Roman Emperor alone.<sup id="cite_ref-Eccardt_2005_176_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eccardt_2005_176-18">[18]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The family, from which the principality takes its name, originally came from <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_Castle" class="mw-redirect" title="Liechtenstein Castle">Liechtenstein Castle</a> in Lower Austria which they had possessed from at least 1140 until the 13th century (and again from 1807 onwards). The Liechtensteins acquired land, predominantly in <a href="/wiki/Moravia" title="Moravia">Moravia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lower_Austria" title="Lower Austria">Lower Austria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">Silesia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Styria" title="Duchy of Styria">Styria</a>. As these territories were all held in <a href="/wiki/Fee_(feudal_tenure)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fee (feudal tenure)">feudal tenure</a> from more senior feudal lords, particularly various branches of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Habsburg" title="House of Habsburg">Habsburgs</a>, the Liechtenstein dynasty was unable to meet a primary requirement to qualify for a seat in the Imperial diet (parliament), the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Diet_(Holy_Roman_Empire)" title="Imperial Diet (Holy Roman Empire)"><i>Reichstag</i></a>. Even though several Liechtenstein princes served several Habsburg rulers as close advisers, without any territory held directly from the Imperial throne, they held little power in the Holy Roman Empire. </p>
      <p>For this reason, the family sought to acquire lands that would be classed as <i>unmittelbar</i> (not sellable) or held without any intermediate feudal tenure, directly from the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a>. During the early 17th century <a href="/wiki/Karl_I,_Prince_of_Liechtenstein" title="Karl I, Prince of Liechtenstein">Karl I of Liechtenstein</a> was made a <i>Fürst</i> (prince) by the Holy Roman Emperor <a href="/wiki/Matthias,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Matthias, Holy Roman Emperor">Matthias</a> after siding with him in a political battle. <a href="/wiki/Hans-Adam_I,_Prince_of_Liechtenstein" class="mw-redirect" title="Hans-Adam I, Prince of Liechtenstein">Hans-Adam I</a> was allowed to purchase the minuscule <a href="/wiki/Lordship_of_Schellenberg" title="Lordship of Schellenberg"><i>Herrschaft</i> ("Lordship") of Schellenberg</a> and the <a href="/wiki/County_of_Vaduz" title="County of Vaduz">county of Vaduz</a> (in 1699 and 1712 respectively) from the Hohenems. Tiny Schellenberg and Vaduz had exactly the political status required: no feudal lord other than their <a href="/wiki/Count" title="Count">comital</a> sovereign and the <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerain</a> Emperor. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Principality">Principality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Principality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>On 23 January 1719,<sup id="cite_ref-creation_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-creation-19">[19]</a></sup> after the lands had been purchased, <a href="/wiki/Charles_VI,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles VI, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles VI, Holy Roman Emperor</a>, decreed that Vaduz and Schellenberg were united and elevated the newly formed territory to the dignity of <i>Fürstentum</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Principality" title="Principality">principality</a></i>) with the name "Liechtenstein" in honour of "[his] true servant, <a href="/wiki/Anton_Florian,_Prince_of_Liechtenstein" title="Anton Florian, Prince of Liechtenstein">Anton Florian of Liechtenstein</a>". It was on this date that Liechtenstein became a sovereign member state of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>. It is a testimony to the mere political expediency of the purchase that the Princes of Liechtenstein did not visit their new principality for almost 100 years. </p>
      <p>By the early 19th century, as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> in Europe, the Holy Roman Empire came under the effective control of France, following the crushing defeat at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Austerlitz" title="Battle of Austerlitz">Austerlitz</a> by <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> in 1805. Emperor <a href="/wiki/Francis_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Francis II, Holy Roman Emperor">Francis II</a> abdicated, ending more than 960 years of feudal government. Napoleon reorganized much of the Empire into the <a href="/wiki/Confederation_of_the_Rhine" title="Confederation of the Rhine">Confederation of the Rhine</a>. This political restructuring had broad consequences for Liechtenstein: the historical imperial, legal, and political institutions had been dissolved. The state ceased to owe an obligation to any feudal lord beyond its borders.<sup id="cite_ref-creation_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-creation-19">[19]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Modern publications generally attribute Liechtenstein's sovereignty to these events. Its prince ceased to owe an obligation to any <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerain</a>. From 25 July 1806, when the <a href="/wiki/Confederation_of_the_Rhine" title="Confederation of the Rhine">Confederation of the Rhine</a> was founded, the Prince of Liechtenstein was a member, in fact, a vassal, of its <a href="/wiki/Hegemon" class="mw-redirect" title="Hegemon">hegemon</a>, styled <i>protector</i>, the French Emperor Napoleon I, until the dissolution of the confederation on 19 October 1813. </p>
      <p>Soon afterward, Liechtenstein joined the <a href="/wiki/German_Confederation" title="German Confederation">German Confederation</a> (20 June 1815&nbsp;– 24 August 1866), which was presided over by the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Austria" title="Emperor of Austria">Emperor of Austria</a>. </p>
      <p>In 1818, <a href="/wiki/Johann_I_Joseph,_Prince_of_Liechtenstein" class="mw-redirect" title="Johann I Joseph, Prince of Liechtenstein">Prince Johann I</a> granted the territory a limited constitution. In that same year <a href="/wiki/Aloys_II,_Prince_of_Liechtenstein" class="mw-redirect" title="Aloys II, Prince of Liechtenstein">Prince Aloys</a> became the first member of the House of Liechtenstein to set foot in the principality that bore their name. The next visit would not occur until 1842. </p>
      <p>Developments during the 19th century included: </p> 
      <ul>
       <li>1836: the first factory, for making ceramics, was opened.</li> 
       <li>1861: the Savings and Loans Bank was founded along with the first cotton-weaving mill.</li> 
       <li>1866: the German Confederation was dissolved.</li> 
       <li>1868: the Liechtenstein Army was disbanded for financial reasons.</li> 
       <li>1872: a railway line between Switzerland and the Austro-Hungarian Empire was constructed through Liechtenstein.</li> 
       <li>1886: two bridges over the Rhine to Switzerland were built.</li>
      </ul> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Until the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, Liechtenstein was closely tied first to the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">Austrian Empire</a> and later to <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a>; the ruling princes continued to derive much of their wealth from estates in the Habsburg territories, and spent much of their time at their two palaces in Vienna. <a href="/wiki/Johann_II,_Prince_of_Liechtenstein" title="Johann II, Prince of Liechtenstein">Johann II</a> appointed <a href="/wiki/Carl_von_In_der_Maur" title="Carl von In der Maur">Carl von In der Maur</a>, an Austrian aristocrat, to serve as the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Liechtenstein" class="mw-redirect" title="Prime Minister of Liechtenstein">Governor of Liechtenstein</a>. The economic devastation caused by the war forced the country to conclude a <a href="/w/index.php?title=Swiss_Customs_Area&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Swiss Customs Area (page does not exist)">customs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monetary_union" class="mw-redirect" title="Monetary union">monetary union</a> with its other neighbour, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Franz_I_von_Liechtenstein_in_Russia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Franz_I_von_Liechtenstein_in_Russia.jpg/170px-Franz_I_von_Liechtenstein_in_Russia.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Franz_I_von_Liechtenstein_in_Russia.jpg/255px-Franz_I_von_Liechtenstein_in_Russia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Franz_I_von_Liechtenstein_in_Russia.jpg/340px-Franz_I_von_Liechtenstein_in_Russia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1357" data-file-height="1858"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Franz_I_von_Liechtenstein_in_Russia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Franz_I,_Prince_of_Liechtenstein" title="Franz I, Prince of Liechtenstein">Franz I, Prince of Liechtenstein</a> from 1929 to 1938
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1929, 75-year-old <a href="/wiki/Franz_I,_Prince_of_Liechtenstein" title="Franz I, Prince of Liechtenstein">Prince Franz I</a> succeeded to the throne. He had just married <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_von_Gutmann" title="Elisabeth von Gutmann">Elisabeth von Gutmann</a>, a wealthy woman from Vienna whose father was a Jewish businessman from Moravia. Although Liechtenstein had no official Nazi party, a Nazi sympathy movement arose within its National Union party. Local Liechtenstein Nazis identified Elisabeth as their Jewish "problem".<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In March 1938, just after the <a href="/wiki/Anschluss" title="Anschluss">annexation of Austria by Nazi Germany</a>, Franz named as regent his 31-year-old grandnephew and heir-presumptive, <a href="/wiki/Franz_Joseph_II,_Prince_of_Liechtenstein" title="Franz Joseph II, Prince of Liechtenstein">Prince Franz Joseph</a>. Franz died in July that year, and Franz Joseph succeeded to the throne. Franz Joseph II first moved to Liechtenstein in 1938, a few days after Austria's annexation.<sup id="cite_ref-Eccardt_2005_176_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eccardt_2005_176-18">[18]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Liechtenstein remained officially neutral, looking to neighbouring Switzerland for assistance and guidance, while family treasures from dynastic lands and possessions in <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moravia" title="Moravia">Moravia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">Silesia</a> were taken to Liechtenstein for safekeeping. At the close of the conflict, <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, acting to seize what they considered German possessions, expropriated all of the Liechtenstein dynasty's properties in those three regions. The expropriations (subject to <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Liechtenstein#International_dispute_with_the_Czech_Republic_and_Slovakia" title="Foreign relations of Liechtenstein">modern legal dispute</a> at the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a>) included over 1,600&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (618&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi) of agricultural and forest land (most notably the UNESCO listed <a href="/wiki/Lednice%E2%80%93Valtice_Cultural_Landscape" title="Lednice–Valtice Cultural Landscape">Lednice–Valtice Cultural Landscape</a>), and several family castles and palaces. </p>
      <p>In 2005 it was revealed that Jewish labourers from the <a href="/wiki/Strasshof_an_der_Nordbahn" title="Strasshof an der Nordbahn">Strasshof</a> <a href="/wiki/Concentration_camp" class="mw-redirect" title="Concentration camp">concentration camp</a>, provided by the <i><a href="/wiki/Schutzstaffel" title="Schutzstaffel">SS</a></i>, had worked on estates in Austria owned by Liechtenstein's Princely House.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Citizens of Liechtenstein were forbidden to enter Czechoslovakia during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. More recently the diplomatic conflict revolving around the controversial postwar <a href="/wiki/Bene%C5%A1_decrees" title="Beneš decrees">Beneš decrees</a> resulted in Liechtenstein not having international relations with the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>. Diplomatic relations were established between Liechtenstein and the Czech Republic on 13 July 2009,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> and with Slovakia on 9 December 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_centre">Financial centre</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Financial centre">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Liechtenstein was in dire financial straits following the end of the war in Europe. The Liechtenstein dynasty often resorted to selling family artistic treasures, including the portrait <i><a href="/wiki/Ginevra_de%27_Benci" title="Ginevra de' Benci">Ginevra de' Benci</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>, which was purchased by the <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Art" title="National Gallery of Art">National Gallery of Art</a> of the United States in 1967 for <span style="white-space: nowrap"><a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a></span>5&nbsp;million ($39 million in 2020 dollars), then a record price for a painting. </p>
      <p>By the late 1970s, Liechtenstein used its low <a href="/wiki/Corporate_tax" title="Corporate tax">corporate tax</a> rates to draw many companies and became one of the wealthiest countries in the world. </p>
      <p>Liechtenstein is one of the only in Europe (along with Monaco and San Marino) to not have a tax treaty with the United States, and efforts toward one seem to have stalled.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As of September&nbsp;2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Liechtenstein" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince of Liechtenstein">Prince of Liechtenstein</a> is the world's sixth <a href="/wiki/List_of_the_richest_royals" class="mw-redirect" title="List of the richest royals">wealthiest monarch</a>, with an estimated wealth of <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$</span>3.5&nbsp;billion.<sup id="cite_ref-CEOWorld2019_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CEOWorld2019-28">[28]</a></sup> The country's population enjoys one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">world's highest standards of living</a>. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Liechtenstein" title="Politics of Liechtenstein">Politics of Liechtenstein</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Liechtenstein_-_Gemeinden_mit_Exklaven.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Liechtenstein_-_Gemeinden_mit_Exklaven.png/260px-Liechtenstein_-_Gemeinden_mit_Exklaven.png" decoding="async" width="260" height="422" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Liechtenstein_-_Gemeinden_mit_Exklaven.png/390px-Liechtenstein_-_Gemeinden_mit_Exklaven.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Liechtenstein_-_Gemeinden_mit_Exklaven.png/520px-Liechtenstein_-_Gemeinden_mit_Exklaven.png 2x" data-file-width="1359" data-file-height="2206"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Liechtenstein_-_Gemeinden_mit_Exklaven.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Administrative divisions of Liechtenstein
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Landtagsgeb%C3%A4ude_und_Regierungsgeb%C3%A4ude.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Landtagsgeb%C3%A4ude_und_Regierungsgeb%C3%A4ude.jpg/220px-Landtagsgeb%C3%A4ude_und_Regierungsgeb%C3%A4ude.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Landtagsgeb%C3%A4ude_und_Regierungsgeb%C3%A4ude.jpg/330px-Landtagsgeb%C3%A4ude_und_Regierungsgeb%C3%A4ude.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Landtagsgeb%C3%A4ude_und_Regierungsgeb%C3%A4ude.jpg/440px-Landtagsgeb%C3%A4ude_und_Regierungsgeb%C3%A4ude.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3543" data-file-height="2357"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Landtagsgeb%C3%A4ude_und_Regierungsgeb%C3%A4ude.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The centre of government in <a href="/wiki/Vaduz" title="Vaduz">Vaduz</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Hans-Adam_Prince_of_Liechtenstein_(1974)_by_Erling_Mandelmann.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Hans-Adam_Prince_of_Liechtenstein_%281974%29_by_Erling_Mandelmann.jpg/170px-Hans-Adam_Prince_of_Liechtenstein_%281974%29_by_Erling_Mandelmann.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e8/Hans-Adam_Prince_of_Liechtenstein_%281974%29_by_Erling_Mandelmann.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="201" data-file-height="300"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Hans-Adam_Prince_of_Liechtenstein_(1974)_by_Erling_Mandelmann.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Hans-Adam_II,_Prince_of_Liechtenstein" title="Hans-Adam II, Prince of Liechtenstein">Hans-Adam II, Prince of Liechtenstein</a>, as pictured by <a href="/wiki/Erling_Mandelmann" title="Erling Mandelmann">Erling Mandelmann</a> in 1974
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Liechtenstein has a <a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">monarch</a> as <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">Head of State</a>, and an elected <a href="/wiki/Parliament" title="Parliament">parliament</a> that enacts the law. It is also a <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a>, where voters can propose and enact constitutional amendments and <a href="/wiki/Legislation" title="Legislation">legislation</a> independently of the legislature.<sup id="cite_ref-Marxer_2007_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marxer_2007-29">[29]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Liechtenstein" title="Constitution of Liechtenstein">Constitution of Liechtenstein</a> was <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_constitutional_referendum,_2003" class="mw-redirect" title="Liechtenstein constitutional referendum, 2003">adopted in March 2003</a>, replacing the 1921 constitution. The 1921 constitution had established Liechtenstein as a constitutional monarchy headed by the reigning prince of the Princely House of Liechtenstein; a parliamentary system had been established, although the reigning Prince retained substantial political authority. </p>
      <p>The reigning Prince is the Head of State and represents Liechtenstein in its international relations (although Switzerland has taken responsibility for much of Liechtenstein's diplomatic relations). The Prince may veto laws adopted by parliament. The Prince may call referendums, propose new legislation, and dissolve parliament, although dissolution of parliament may be subject to a referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Executive authority is vested in a collegiate <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a> comprising the head of government (<a href="/wiki/Prime_minister" title="Prime minister">prime minister</a>) and four government councillors (ministers). The head of government and the other ministers are appointed by the Prince upon the proposal of parliament and with its concurrence, and reflect the balance of parties in parliament. The constitution stipulates that at least two members of the government be chosen from each of the two regions.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The members of the government are collectively and individually responsible to parliament; parliament may ask the Prince to remove an individual minister or the entire government. </p>
      <p>Legislative authority is vested in the unicameral <a href="/wiki/Landtag_of_Liechtenstein" title="Landtag of Liechtenstein">Landtag</a>, made up of 25 members elected for maximum four-year terms according to a proportional representation formula. Fifteen members are elected from the <a href="/wiki/Oberland_(electoral_district)" title="Oberland (electoral district)">Oberland</a> (Upper Country or region) and ten from the <a href="/wiki/Unterland_(electoral_district)" title="Unterland (electoral district)">Unterland</a> (Lower Country or region).<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Parties must receive at least 8% of the national vote to win seats in parliament, i.e. enough for 2 seats in the 25-seat legislature. Parliament proposes and approves a government, which is formally appointed by the Prince. Parliament may also pass votes of no confidence in the entire government or individual members. </p>
      <p>Parliament elects from among its members a "Landesausschuss" (National Committee) made up of the president of the parliament and four additional members. The National Committee is charged with performing functions of parliamentary supervision. Parliament can call for <a href="/wiki/Referendum" title="Referendum">referendums</a> on proposed legislation. Parliament shares the authority to propose new legislation with the Prince and with the number of <a href="/wiki/Citizenship" title="Citizenship">citizens</a> required for to initiate a referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Judicial authority is vested in the Regional Court at Vaduz, the Princely High Court of Appeal at Vaduz, the Princely Supreme Court, the Administrative Court, and the State Court. The State Court rules on the conformity of laws with the constitution and has five members elected by parliament. </p>
      <p>On 1 July 1984, Liechtenstein became the last country in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> to grant women the right to vote. The <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_women%27s_suffrage_referendum,_1984" class="mw-redirect" title="Liechtenstein women's suffrage referendum, 1984">referendum on women's suffrage</a>, in which only men were allowed to participate, passed with 51.3% in favour.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_constitution">New constitution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: New constitution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In a <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_constitutional_referendum,_2003" class="mw-redirect" title="Liechtenstein constitutional referendum, 2003">national referendum</a> in March 2003, nearly two-thirds of the electorate voted in support of Hans-Adam II's proposed new constitution. The proposed constitution was criticised by many, including the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a>, as expanding the powers of the monarchy (continuing the power to veto any law, and allowing the Prince to dismiss the government or any minister). The Prince threatened that if the constitution failed, he would, among other things, convert some royal property for commercial use and move to Austria.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The princely family and the Prince enjoy tremendous public support inside the nation, and the resolution passed with about 64% in favour.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> A proposal to revoke the Prince's veto powers was rejected by 76% of voters in a <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_constitutional_referendum,_2012" class="mw-redirect" title="Liechtenstein constitutional referendum, 2012">2012 referendum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Municipalities">Municipalities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Municipalities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Liechtenstein" title="Municipalities of Liechtenstein">Municipalities in Liechtenstein</a> are entitled to secede from the union by majority vote.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The municipalities of Liechtenstein are divided between the two electoral <a href="/wiki/Districts" class="mw-redirect" title="Districts">districts</a> of Unterland and Oberland. The political division of the country is historical; the Unterland depends on Schellenberg, the Oberland on the county of Vaduz. </p>
      <p>The communities Eschen, Gamprin, Mauren, Ruggell and Schellenberg belong to Unterland; the municipalities of Balzers, Planken, Schaan, Triesen, Triesenberg and Vaduz belong to the much larger Oberland. The autonomy of the Liechtenstein communities is in the upper range compared to the other Central European states along with Switzerland. Despite their small size, the municipalities have complex forms in terms of their territorial extent. In addition to a main part, seven municipalities also comprise one or more <a href="/wiki/Enclaves" class="mw-redirect" title="Enclaves">enclaves</a>. Citizens' cooperatives, which exist in about half of Liechtenstein's municipalities, own forests and pastures for <a href="/wiki/Collective_farming" title="Collective farming">collective use</a>, as well as parceled areas that are left for private use. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_awards">International awards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: International awards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In 2013, Liechtenstein won for the first time a SolarSuperState Prize in the category Solar recognizing the achieved level of the usage of photovoltaics per population within the state territory.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The SolarSuperState Association justified this prize with the cumulative installed photovoltaic power of some 290 Watt per capita at the end of 2012. This placed Liechtenstein second in the world after Germany. Also in 2014, the SolarSuperState Association awarded the second place SolarSuperState Prize in the category Solar to Liechtenstein.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> In the years 2015 and 2016, Liechtenstein was honoured with the first place SolarSuperState Prize in the category Solar because it had the world's biggest cumulative installed photovoltaic power per population.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Liechtenstein" title="Geography of Liechtenstein">Geography of Liechtenstein</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Rhein_bei_Balzers_-_Blick_auf_Gonzen.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Rhein_bei_Balzers_-_Blick_auf_Gonzen.JPG/220px-Rhein_bei_Balzers_-_Blick_auf_Gonzen.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Rhein_bei_Balzers_-_Blick_auf_Gonzen.JPG/330px-Rhein_bei_Balzers_-_Blick_auf_Gonzen.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Rhein_bei_Balzers_-_Blick_auf_Gonzen.JPG/440px-Rhein_bei_Balzers_-_Blick_auf_Gonzen.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Rhein_bei_Balzers_-_Blick_auf_Gonzen.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a>: border between Liechtenstein and Switzerland (view towards the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Alps" title="Swiss Alps">Swiss Alps</a>)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Liechtenstein is situated in the Upper <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> valley of the European <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a> and is bordered to the east by the Austrian region of <a href="/wiki/Vorarlberg" title="Vorarlberg">Vorarlberg</a> and to the south by the <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Grisons" class="mw-redirect" title="Canton of Grisons">canton of Grisons</a> (Switzerland) and to the west by the <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_St._Gallen" title="Canton of St. Gallen">canton of St. Gallen</a> (Switzerland). The entire western border of Liechtenstein is formed by the Rhine. Measured south to north the country is about 24&nbsp;km (15&nbsp;mi) long. Its highest point, the <a href="/wiki/Grauspitz" title="Grauspitz">Grauspitz</a>, is 2,599&nbsp;m (8,527&nbsp;ft). Despite its Alpine location, prevailing southerly winds make the climate comparatively mild. In winter, the mountain slopes are well suited to winter sports. </p>
      <p>New <a href="/wiki/Surveying" title="Surveying">surveys</a> using more accurate measurements of the country's borders in 2006 have set its area at 160&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (62&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi), with borders of 77.9&nbsp;km (48.4&nbsp;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Liechtenstein's borders are 1.9&nbsp;km (1.2&nbsp;mi) longer than previously thought.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Liechtenstein is one of the world's two <a href="/wiki/Doubly_landlocked_country" class="mw-redirect" title="Doubly landlocked country">doubly landlocked countries</a><sup id="cite_ref-LiechCIA_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LiechCIA-45">[45]</a></sup> — countries wholly surrounded by other landlocked countries (the other is <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>). Liechtenstein is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area" title="List of countries and dependencies by area">sixth-smallest</a> independent nation in the world by area. </p>
      <p>The principality of Liechtenstein is <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Liechtenstein" title="Municipalities of Liechtenstein">divided into 11 communes</a> called <i>Gemeinden</i> (singular <i>Gemeinde</i>). The <i>Gemeinden</i> mostly consist of only a single town or village. Five of them (<a href="/wiki/Eschen" title="Eschen">Eschen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gamprin" title="Gamprin">Gamprin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauren" title="Mauren">Mauren</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ruggell" title="Ruggell">Ruggell</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Schellenberg" title="Schellenberg">Schellenberg</a>) fall within the electoral district <i>Unterland</i> (the lower county), and the remainder (<a href="/wiki/Balzers" title="Balzers">Balzers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Planken" title="Planken">Planken</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schaan" title="Schaan">Schaan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Triesen" title="Triesen">Triesen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Triesenberg" title="Triesenberg">Triesenberg</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vaduz" title="Vaduz">Vaduz</a>) within <i>Oberland</i> (the upper county). </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Despite its <a href="/wiki/Alpine_climate" title="Alpine climate">alpine</a> location, the prevailing southerly winds temper Liechtenstein's climate. Its climate is continental, with cloudy and cold winters, with frequent rain and snowfall. Summers are cool to slightly warm, cloudy and humid. </p>
      <p>The country's climate is relatively mild despite its mountainous location. It is strongly influenced by the action of <a href="/wiki/Foehn" class="mw-redirect" title="Foehn">foehn</a> (warm and dry autumn wind), so the vegetation period is prolonged in spring and autumn and temperatures around 15 ° C due to the strong foehn are not uncommon even in winter. The mountain ranges of <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> and Vorarlberg upstream protect from the cold polar and Atlantic air, creating a typical alpine inland protective layer. The principality has orchards with leafy meadows and a long tradition of viticulture. Liechtenstein's small land area hardly plays a role in climatic differences, but the vertical division into different altitudes is of great importance, so that significant climatic differences arise. </p>
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Winter" title="Winter">winter</a> the temperature rarely drops below minus 15 degrees Celsius, while in summer the average temperatures range between 20 and 28 degrees Celsius. Annual precipitation measurements amount to an average of about 900 to 1200 millimeters, in the direct alpine region, however, <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a> is often up to 1900 millimeters. The average duration of insolation is about 1600 hours per year. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Vaduz,_Liechtenstein._Schweize_Gebirge_im_Hintergrund_-_panoramio.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Vaduz%2C_Liechtenstein._Schweize_Gebirge_im_Hintergrund_-_panoramio.jpg/300px-Vaduz%2C_Liechtenstein._Schweize_Gebirge_im_Hintergrund_-_panoramio.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Vaduz%2C_Liechtenstein._Schweize_Gebirge_im_Hintergrund_-_panoramio.jpg/450px-Vaduz%2C_Liechtenstein._Schweize_Gebirge_im_Hintergrund_-_panoramio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Vaduz%2C_Liechtenstein._Schweize_Gebirge_im_Hintergrund_-_panoramio.jpg/600px-Vaduz%2C_Liechtenstein._Schweize_Gebirge_im_Hintergrund_-_panoramio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1080"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Vaduz,_Liechtenstein._Schweize_Gebirge_im_Hintergrund_-_panoramio.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Panorama of <a href="/wiki/Vaduz" title="Vaduz">Vaduz</a>, capital of Liechtenstein
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rivers_and_lakes">Rivers and lakes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Rivers and lakes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> is the longest and largest <a href="/wiki/Body_of_water" title="Body of water">body of water</a> in Liechtenstein. With a length of approximately 27 kilometers, it represents the <a href="/wiki/Natural_border" title="Natural border">natural border</a> with <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> and is of great importance for Liechtenstein's water supply. Furthermore, the Rhine is an important recreational area for the population. At 10 kilometers, the Samina is the second longest river in the <a href="/wiki/Principality" title="Principality">Principality</a>. The troubled river begins at Triesenberg and flows into the Ill in Austria (near Feldkirch). </p>
      <p>The only naturally formed lake in Liechtenstein is the Gampriner Seelein, which was not formed until 1927 by a <a href="/wiki/Flooding" class="mw-redirect" title="Flooding">flooding</a> of the Rhine with enormous erosion. In addition, there are other artificially created <a href="/wiki/Lakes" class="mw-redirect" title="Lakes">lakes</a>, which are mainly used to generate <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>. One of them is the Steg Reservoir, the largest lake in Liechtenstein. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mountains">Mountains</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Mountains">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>About half of Liechtenstein's territory is mountainous.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-46">[46]</a></sup> Liechtenstein lies entirely in the <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A4tikon" title="Rätikon">Rhaetikon</a> and is thus - depending on the classification of the Alps - assigned to the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Alps" title="Eastern Alps">Eastern Alps</a> (two-part division of the Alps) or the Central Alps (three-part division of the Alps). </p>
      <p>The highest point of Liechtenstein is the Vordere Grauspitz (Vordergrauspitz) with an altitude of 2599 m <a href="/wiki/Above_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Above sea level">above sea level</a>, while the lowest point is the Ruggeller Riet with an altitude of 430 m above sea level. </p>
      <p>In total, there are 32 mountains in Liechtenstein with an altitude of at least 2000 <a href="/wiki/Meters" class="mw-redirect" title="Meters">meters</a>. The Falknishorn, at 2452 meters above sea level, is the fifth highest mountain in Liechtenstein and represents the southernmost point of the country. The Liechtenstein-Graubünden-Vorarlberg border triangle is the <a href="/wiki/Naafkopf" title="Naafkopf">Naafkopf</a> (2570 m above sea level).<sup id="cite_ref-:0_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-46">[46]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In addition to the peaks of the Alpine chain,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> which belong to the Limestone Alps, two inselbergs, Fläscherberg (1135 <a href="/wiki/Meters_above_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Meters above sea level">m a.s.l</a>.) in the south and <a href="/wiki/Eschnerberg" title="Eschnerberg">Eschnerberg</a> (698 m a.s.l.) in the north, rise from the Rhine Valley and belong to the Helvetic cover or flysch zone of the Alps.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Eschnerberg represents an important settlement area in the Liechtenstein Unterland. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Liechtenstein" title="Economy of Liechtenstein">Economy of Liechtenstein</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Vaduz_Zentrum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Vaduz_Zentrum.jpg/220px-Vaduz_Zentrum.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Vaduz_Zentrum.jpg/330px-Vaduz_Zentrum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Vaduz_Zentrum.jpg/440px-Vaduz_Zentrum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3735" data-file-height="2773"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Vaduz_Zentrum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Looking southward at Vaduz city centre
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Despite its limited natural resources, Liechtenstein is one of the few countries in the world with more registered companies than citizens; it has developed a prosperous, highly industrialized free-enterprise economy and boasts a financial service sector as well as a living standard that compares favourably with those of the urban areas of Liechtenstein's much larger European neighbours. </p>
      <p>Liechtenstein participates in a <a href="/wiki/Customs_union" title="Customs union">customs union</a> with Switzerland and employs the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_franc" title="Swiss franc">Swiss franc</a> as the national currency. The country imports about 85% of its energy. Liechtenstein has been a member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a> (an organization serving as a bridge between the <a href="/wiki/European_Free_Trade_Association" title="European Free Trade Association">European Free Trade Association</a> (EFTA) and the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>) since May 1995. </p>
      <p>The government is working to harmonize its economic policies with those of an integrated Europe. In 2008, the unemployment rate stood at 1.5%. Liechtenstein has only one hospital, the Liechtensteinisches Landesspital in Vaduz. As of 2014 the <a href="/wiki/CIA_World_Factbook" class="mw-redirect" title="CIA World Factbook">CIA World Factbook</a> estimated the gross domestic product (GDP) on a <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a> basis to be $4.978&nbsp;billion. As of 2009 the estimate per capita was $139,100, the highest listed for the world.<sup id="cite_ref-LiechCIA_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LiechCIA-45">[45]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Industries include electronics, textiles, precision instruments, metal manufacturing, power tools, anchor bolts, calculators, pharmaceuticals, and food products. Its most recognizable international company and largest employer is <a href="/wiki/Hilti" title="Hilti">Hilti</a>, a manufacturer of <a href="/wiki/Nail_gun" title="Nail gun">direct fastening systems</a> and other high-end power tools. Many cultivated fields and small farms are found both in the Oberland and Unterland. Liechtenstein produces wheat, barley, corn, potatoes, dairy products, livestock, and <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_wine" title="Liechtenstein wine">wine</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxation">Taxation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Taxation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Grenze_Liechtenstein,_Balzers.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Grenze_Liechtenstein%2C_Balzers.jpg/220px-Grenze_Liechtenstein%2C_Balzers.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Grenze_Liechtenstein%2C_Balzers.jpg/330px-Grenze_Liechtenstein%2C_Balzers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Grenze_Liechtenstein%2C_Balzers.jpg/440px-Grenze_Liechtenstein%2C_Balzers.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3257" data-file-height="2395"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Grenze_Liechtenstein,_Balzers.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Since 1923, there has been no <a href="/wiki/Border_control" title="Border control">border control</a> between Liechtenstein and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The government of Liechtenstein taxes personal income, business income, and principal (wealth). The basic rate of <a href="/wiki/Income_tax#Personal" title="Income tax">personal income tax</a> is 1.2%. When combined with the additional income tax imposed by the communes, the combined income tax rate is 17.82%.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> An additional income tax of 4.3% is levied on all employees under the country's <a href="/wiki/Social_security" class="mw-redirect" title="Social security">social security</a> programme. This rate is higher for the self-employed, up to a maximum of 11%, making the maximum income tax rate about 29% in total. The basic <a href="/wiki/Wealth_tax" title="Wealth tax">tax rate on wealth</a> is 0.06% per annum, and the combined total rate is 0.89%. The tax rate on corporate profits is 12.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-LiechCIA_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LiechCIA-45">[45]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Liechtenstein's <a href="/wiki/Gift_tax" title="Gift tax">gift</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inheritance_tax" title="Inheritance tax">estate taxes</a> vary depending on the relationship the recipient has to the giver and the amount of the inheritance. The tax ranges between 0.5% and 0.75% for spouses and children and 18% to 27% for non-related recipients. The estate tax is progressive. </p>
      <p>Liechtenstein has previously received significant revenues from <i>Stiftungen</i> ("foundations"), financial entities created to hide the true owner of nonresident foreigners' financial holdings. The foundation is registered in the name of a Liechtensteiner, often a lawyer. This set of laws used to make Liechtenstein a popular <a href="/wiki/Tax_haven" title="Tax haven">tax haven</a> for extremely wealthy individuals and businesses attempting to avoid or evade taxes in their home countries.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In recent years, Liechtenstein has displayed stronger determination to prosecute international money launderers and worked to promote an image as a legitimate finance centre. In February 2008, the country's <a href="/wiki/LGT_Group" title="LGT Group">LGT Bank</a> was implicated in a <a href="/wiki/2008_Liechtenstein_tax_affair" title="2008 Liechtenstein tax affair">tax-fraud scandal in Germany</a>, which strained the ruling family's relationship with the German government. Crown Prince Alois has accused the German government of trafficking in stolen goods, referring to its $7.3&nbsp;million purchase of private banking information offered by a former employee of LGT Group.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senate</a>'s subcommittee on tax haven banks said that the LGT bank, owned by the princely family, and on whose board they serve, "is a willing partner, and an aider and abettor to clients trying to evade taxes, dodge creditors or defy court orders".<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Hilti_Schaan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hilti_Schaan.jpg/220px-Hilti_Schaan.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hilti_Schaan.jpg/330px-Hilti_Schaan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hilti_Schaan.jpg/440px-Hilti_Schaan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2244" data-file-height="1488"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Hilti_Schaan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Headquarters of <a href="/wiki/Hilti" title="Hilti">Hilti</a> Corporation in <a href="/wiki/Schaan" title="Schaan">Schaan</a>, Liechtenstein
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/2008_Liechtenstein_tax_affair" title="2008 Liechtenstein tax affair">2008 Liechtenstein tax affair</a> is a series of tax investigations in numerous countries whose governments suspect that some of their citizens have evaded tax obligations by using banks and trusts in Liechtenstein; the affair broke open with the biggest complex of investigations ever initiated for tax evasion in Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> It was also seen as an attempt to put pressure on Liechtenstein, then one of the remaining <a href="/wiki/FATF_blacklist" title="FATF blacklist">uncooperative tax havens</a>—along with <a href="/wiki/Andorra" title="Andorra">Andorra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monaco" title="Monaco">Monaco</a>—as identified by the Paris-based <a href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development</a> in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj0219_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj0219-55">[55]</a></sup> On 27 May 2009 the OECD removed Liechtenstein from the blacklist of uncooperative countries.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In August 2009, the British government department <a href="/wiki/HM_Revenue_%26_Customs" class="mw-redirect" title="HM Revenue &amp; Customs">HM Revenue &amp; Customs</a> agreed with Liechtenstein to start exchanging information. It is believed that up to 5,000 British investors have roughly £3&nbsp;billion deposited in accounts and trusts in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In October 2015, the European Union and Liechtenstein signed a tax agreement to ensure the automatic exchange of financial information in case of tax disputes. The collection of data started in 2016, and is another step to bring the principality in line with other European countries with regard to its taxation of private individuals and corporate assets.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Tourism accounts for a large portion of Liechtenstein's economy. Indeed, <a href="/wiki/Airbnb" title="Airbnb">Airbnb</a> once offered the ability to rent space for 450-900 guests in Liechtenstein for about US$70,000 per night.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Liechtenstein" title="Demographics of Liechtenstein">Demographics of Liechtenstein</a>
      </div> 
      <p>As for population, Liechtenstein is Europe's fourth-smallest country; <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican City</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Marino" title="San Marino">San Marino</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Monaco" title="Monaco">Monaco</a> have fewer residents. Its population is primarily <a href="/wiki/Alemannic_German" title="Alemannic German">Alemannic</a>-speaking, although one third is foreign-born, primarily German speakers from Germany, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, along with other Swiss, Italians, and <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turks</a>. Foreign-born people make up two-thirds of the country's workforce.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Liechtensteiners have an average life expectancy at birth of 82.0 years, subdividing as male: 79.8 years, female: 84.8 years (2018 est.). The infant mortality rate is 4.2 deaths per 1,000 live births, according to 2018 estimates. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The official language is German; most speak an <a href="/wiki/Alemannic_German" title="Alemannic German">Alemannic</a> <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialect</a> of German that is highly divergent from <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">Standard German</a> but closely related to dialects spoken in neighbouring regions such as Switzerland and <a href="/wiki/Vorarlberg" title="Vorarlberg">Vorarlberg</a>, Austria. In <a href="/wiki/Triesenberg" title="Triesenberg">Triesenberg</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Walser_German" title="Walser German">Walser German</a> dialect promoted by the municipality is spoken. <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Standard_German" title="Swiss Standard German">Swiss Standard German</a> is also understood and spoken by most Liechtensteiners. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Liechtenstein" title="Religion in Liechtenstein">Religion in Liechtenstein</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Church_vaduz.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Church_vaduz.png/220px-Church_vaduz.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Church_vaduz.png/330px-Church_vaduz.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Church_vaduz.png/440px-Church_vaduz.png 2x" data-file-width="1944" data-file-height="2592"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Church_vaduz.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>St. Florin Catholic Cathedral in Vaduz
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Liechtenstein" title="Constitution of Liechtenstein">Constitution of Liechtenstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a> is its official <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a>: </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>The Catholic Church is the State Church and as such shall enjoy the full protection of the State</p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        — <cite><a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Liechtenstein" title="Constitution of Liechtenstein">Constitution of Liechtenstein</a><sup id="cite_ref-Temperman2010_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Temperman2010-62">[62]</a></sup></cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Liechtenstein offers protection to adherents of all religions, and considers the "religious interests of the people" a priority of the government.<sup id="cite_ref-Temperman2010_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Temperman2010-62">[62]</a></sup> In Liechtenstein schools, although exceptions are allowed, religious education in Roman Catholicism or <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> (either <a href="/wiki/Reformed" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed">Reformed</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lutheran" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a>, or both) is legally required.<sup id="cite_ref-Piano2009_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Piano2009-63">[63]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tax_exemption" title="Tax exemption">Tax exemption</a> is granted by the government to religious organizations.<sup id="cite_ref-Piano2009_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Piano2009-63">[63]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a>, social conflict caused by religious hostilities is low in Liechtenstein, and so is government restriction on the practice of religion.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to the 2010 census, 85.8% of the total population is Christian, of whom 75.9% adhere to the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> faith, constituted in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Archdiocese_of_Vaduz" title="Roman Catholic Archdiocese of Vaduz">Catholic Archdiocese of Vaduz</a>, while 9.6% are either <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a>, mainly organized in the <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Church_in_Liechtenstein" title="Evangelical Church in Liechtenstein">Evangelical Church in Liechtenstein</a> (a <a href="/wiki/United_church" class="mw-redirect" title="United church">United church</a>, Lutheran &amp; Reformed) and the <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Lutheran_Church_in_Liechtenstein" class="mw-redirect" title="Evangelical Lutheran Church in Liechtenstein">Evangelical Lutheran Church in Liechtenstein</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy" title="Orthodoxy">Orthodox</a>, mainly organized in the Christian-Orthodox Church.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The largest minority religion is <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> (5.4% of the total population).<sup id="cite_ref-r2_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-r2-66">[66]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liechtenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Liechtenstein" title="Education in Liechtenstein">Education in Liechtenstein</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:University_of_Liechtenstein.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/University_of_Liechtenstein.JPG/220px-University_of_Liechtenstein.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/University_of_Liechtenstein.JPG/330px-University_of_Liechtenstein.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/University_of_Liechtenstein.JPG/440px-University_of_Liechtenstein.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1984" data-file-height="1488"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:University_of_Liechtenstein.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/University_of_Liechtenstein" title="University of Liechtenstein">University of Liechtenstein</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The literacy rate of Liechtenstein is 100%.<sup id="cite_ref-LiechCIA_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LiechCIA-45">[45]</a></sup> In 2006 <a href="/wiki/Programme_for_International_Student_Assessment" title="Programme for International Student Assessment">Programme for International Student Assessment</a> report, coordinated by the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development</a>, ranked Liechtenstein's education as the 10th best in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In 2012, Liechtenstein had the highest PISA-scores of any European country.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Within Liechtenstein, there are four main centres for higher education: </p> 
      <ul>
       <li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Liechtenstein" title="University of Liechtenstein">University of Liechtenstein</a></li>
